Let‚Äôs define direction vector v = P - A.

For ő©: We have M = (0,0). The line param: L(s) = A + s v. The equation |L(s)|^2 = r^2. Since A is on ő©, |A|^2 = r^2. So plug in:

|A + s v|^2 = |A|^2 + 2 s (A¬∑v) + s^2 |v|^2 = r^2 + 2 s (A¬∑v) + s^2 |v|^2.

Set equal to r^2 ‚Üí 2 s (A¬∑v) + s^2 |v|^2 = 0 ‚Üí s (2 A¬∑v + s |v|^2) = 0. So s = 0 (A) and s = -2 (A¬∑v) / |v|^2. Thus the second intersection E corresponds to s_E = -2 (A¬∑v) / |v|^2. So E = A + s_E v.

Similarly, for őď: center N = (d,0), radius R. Param L(s) = A + s v. Then |L(s) - N|^2 = R^2. Since A is on őď, |A - N|^2 = R^2. So we have:

Thus s (2 (A - N)¬∑v + s |v|^2) = 0 ‚Üí s = 0 (A) and s = -2 ((A - N)¬∑v) / |v|^2. So F = A + s_F v with s_F = -2 ((A - N)¬∑v) / |v|^2.

y0^2 = r^2 - ((d^2 + r^2 - R^2)^2) / (4 d^2) = (4 d^2 r^2 - (d^2 + r^2 - R^2)^2) / (4 d^2).

The numerator can be factored as ( (2dr)^2 - (d^2 + r^2 - R^2)^2 ) = (2dr - (d^2 + r^2 - R^2)) * (2dr + (d^2 + r^2 - R^2)). That's difference of squares.

Compute:

2dr - (d^2 + r^2 - R^2) = -d^2 - r^2 + 2dr + R^2 = -(d^2 - 2dr + r^2) + R^2 = -(d - r)^2 + R^2 = R^2 - (d - r)^2.
Similarly, 2dr + (d^2 + r^2 - R^2) = (d^2 + 2dr + r^2) - R^2 = (d + r)^2 - R^2.

So numerator = (R^2 - (d - r)^2) * ((d + r)^2 - R^2). So

y0^2 = \frac{ (R^2 - (d - r)^2) \cdot ((d + r)^2 - R^2) }{4 d^2 }.

That's neat. So y0 = \frac{ \sqrt{ (R^2 - (d - r)^2) \cdot ((d + r)^2 - R^2) } }{2 d}. But note that (R^2 - (d - r)^2) and ((d + r)^2 - R^2) are both positive because of intersection condition? PC^2 = (x_P - 0)^2 + (y_P - y_C)^2.

Set equal:

(x_P - a)^2 + y_P^2 = x_P^2 + (y_P - y_C)^2.

Expand:

x_P^2 - 2a x_P + a^2 + y_P^2 = x_P^2 + y_P^2 - 2 y_P y_C + y_C^2.

Cancel x_P^2 and y_P^2:

-2a x_P + a^2 = -2 y_P y_C + y_C^2.

Thus 2 y_P y_C - 2a x_P = y_C^2 - a^2.

So 2 y_P y_C - 2a x_P = y_C^2 - a^2.

We can solve for x_P.

x_H = x_P * (y_P - m) / (n - y_P).

Thus H = ( x_P * (y_P - m) / (n - y_P), y_P ).

We have y_P = (y_C + y_D)/2.

We need x_P from PA = PC.

PA^2 = (x_P - a)^2 + y_P^2.
PC^2 = x_P^2 + (y_P - y_C)^2.

Set equal:

(x_P - a)^2 + y_P^2 = x_P^2 + (y_P - y_C)^2.
=> x_P^2 - 2a x_P + a^2 + y_P^2 = x_P^2 + y_P^2 - 2 y_P y_C + y_C^2.
Cancel x_P^2, y_P^2:
-2a x_P + a^2 = -2 y_P y_C + y_C^2.
=> 2 y_P y_C - 2a x_P = y_C^2 - a^2.
=> 2a x_P = 2 y_P y_C - (y_C^2 - a^2) = 2 y_P y_C - y_C^2 + a^2.
Thus

x_P = \frac{2 y_P y_C - y_C^2 + a^2}{2a}.

But note that we could also use D, which would give a similar expression. That's okay.

Now, y_P = (y_C + y_D)/2. So y_P y_C = (y_C + y_D)/2 * y_C = (y_C^2 + y_C y_D)/2. Then 2 y_P y_C = y_C^2 + y_C y_D. So

x_P = \frac{ y_C^2 + y_C y_D - y_C^2 + a^2 }{2a} = \frac{ y_C y_D + a^2 }{2a}.

That's a nice simplification! Because the y_C^2 cancels. So x_P = \frac{ y_C y_D + a^2 }{2a}.

Maybe we can use an inversion with center A that transforms the circles into lines. Then the points B, C, D, P, H, E, F become something like lines. Then the line through H parallel to AP becomes something like a line that is the image of something. Let's explore.

Invert about A with arbitrary radius. Then:

- ő© becomes a line l1 (since ő© passes through A). Similarly, őď becomes a line l2.
- The points B (second intersection) lies on both circles, so B' = B' (image of B) is the intersection point of l1 and l2. So l1 ‚ą© l2 = B'.
- The line MN (centers) becomes a circle (or line) passing through A. Since A is the center of inversion, it's not mapped, but the image of MN (which does not pass through A generally) is a circle passing through A. That's fine.
- C is on ő©, so C' lies on l1. Similarly, D' lies on l2.
- P is circumcenter of ACD. Under inversion, A is the center, so A is not mapped. The triangle ACD becomes a triangle with A removed, but we can consider the image of the circumcircle of ACD. But we also have H, orthocenter of PMN. Under inversion, H becomes some point H'. Not obviously simplifying.

However note that after inversion, the line AP (which passes through A) becomes itself (as a set). So AP is invariant. The points E and F are on AP and on the circles. Under inversion, E' (image of E) lies on l1 and on AP, similarly F' lies on l2 and AP. So we have two lines l1,l2 intersecting at B', and a line AP intersecting them at E' and F'. That's a simple configuration. Also, the circle (BEF) becomes something like a circle through B' (since B is on both circles) and E',F'? Actually B' is the image of B, and E' and F' are images of E and F. Now, we have a lot of algebra. Maybe we can find a more clever synthetic relationship. Let's consider the homothety with center at the internal center of similarity S that maps ő© to őď. Under this homothety, C (on ő©) maps to something? Since C is on ő©, its image is some point on őď. But D is on őď, and S is on MN. The homothety with center S and ratio k = R/r (positive) sends M to N. It sends the circle ő© to őď. Under this homothety, the point A (on ő©) maps to some point A' on őď. But is A' = A? Not necessarily. However, note that A is on both circles. So if a homothety with center S (on MN) maps ő© to őď, then for any point X on ő©, its image X' lies on őď. In particular, for A on ő©, its image A' lies on őď. Since A is also on őď, it could be that A' = A if A lies on the ray from S such that SA' = k SA. But does A lie on that ray? Usually, S is the internal center of similarity, and the points A and B are the two intersections of the circles. There is a known property: The internal center of similarity is the intersection of lines joining corresponding points of the two circles under a homothety that sends one to the other. For intersecting circles, the internal center of similarity lies on the line joining the centers, between them. The lines connecting the centers to the intersection points are not necessarily passing through S. However, there is a property: If two circles intersect, then the internal center of similarity is the midpoint of the common chord? That's not true. Let's compute S coordinates: M=(0,0), N=(d,0). Then add 2 r^2: d^2 + (d + r)R - r^2 + 2 r^2 = d^2 + (d + r)R + r^2.

Then subtract (d^2 + r^2 - R^2): (d^2 + (d + r)R + r^2) - (d^2 + r^2 - R^2) = (d + r)R + R^2.

Thus numerator = R (d + r) + R^2 = R (d + r + R). So

Another idea: Use complex numbers. Let the common chord AB be the imaginary axis (or real). Then M and N are on the real axis. This is similar to our coordinate system. Complex numbers can handle intersections nicely. We can set up:

Line AP: param A + s (P - A). We'll use s as parameter, with s=0 at A, s=1 at P. But we want the second intersection with ő© (circle centered at M (0,0) radius r). That's a quadratic in s. Since A is a root, the other root gives the second intersection. So we can compute E and F using the formula for secant.

Let‚Äôs define direction vector v = P - A.

For ő©: We have M = (0,0). The line param: L(s) = A + s v. The equation |L(s)|^2 = r^2. Since A is on ő©, |A|^2 = r^2. So plug in:

|A + s v|^2 = |A|^2 + 2 s (A¬∑v) + s^2 |v|^2 = r^2 + 2 s (A¬∑v) + s^2 |v|^2.

Set equal to r^2 ‚Üí 2 s (A¬∑v) + s^2 |v|^2 = 0 ‚Üí s (2 A¬∑v + s |v|^2) = 0. So s = 0 (A) and s = -2 (A¬∑v) / |v|^2. Thus the second intersection E corresponds to s_E = -2 (A¬∑v) / |v|^2. So E = A + s_E v.

Similarly, for őď: center N = (d,0), radius R. Param L(s) = A + s v. Then |L(s) - N|^2 = R^2. Since A is on őď, |A - N|^2 = R^2. So we have:

|A - N + s v|^2 = |A - N|^2 + 2 s ((A - N)¬∑v) + s^2 |v|^2 = R^2 + 2 s ((A - N)¬∑v) + s^2 |v|^2 = R^2.

Thus s (2 (A - N)¬∑v + s |v|^2) = 0 ‚Üí s = 0 (A) and s = -2 ((A - N)¬∑v) / |v|^2. So F = A + s_F v with s_F = -2 ((A - N)¬∑v) / |v|^2.

So we can compute E and F coordinates explicitly in terms of A, P, M, N. That's doable.

Then y0^2 = r^2 - x0^2. That simplifies to:

y0^2 = r^2 - ((d^2 + r^2 - R^2)^2) / (4 d^2) = (4 d^2 r^2 - (d^2 + r^2 - R^2)^2) / (4 d^2).

The numerator can be factored as ( (2dr)^2 - (d^2 + r^2 - R^2)^2 ) = (2dr - (d^2 + r^2 - R^2)) * (2dr + (d^2 + r^2 - R^2)). That's difference of squares.

Compute:

2dr - (d^2 + r^2 - R^2) = -d^2 - r^2 + 2dr + R^2 = -(d^2 - 2dr + r^2) + R^2 = -(d - r)^2 + R^2 = R^2 - (d - r)^2.
Similarly, 2dr + (d^2 + r^2 - R^2) = (d^2 + 2dr + r^2) - R^2 = (d + r)^2 - R^2.

So numerator = (R^2 - (d - r)^2) * ((d + r)^2 - R^2). So

y0^2 = \frac{ (R^2 - (d - r)^2) \cdot ((d + r)^2 - R^2) }{4 d^2 }.

That's neat. So y0 = \frac{ \sqrt{ (R^2 - (d - r)^2) \cdot ((d + r)^2 - R^2) } }{2 d}. But note that (R^2 - (d - r)^2) and ((d + r)^2 - R^2) are both positive because of intersection condition? Let's verify: For intersecting circles, we need d > |R - r| (so d > R - r) and d < R + r. Then (d - r)^2 < R^2? Since d > R - r implies d - r > R - 2r? That's not directly. But we know that R^2 - (d - r)^2 > 0? Let's check: R^2 - (d - r)^2 = (R - (d - r)) (R + (d - r)) = (R - d + r)(R + d - r). Since d < R + r, we have R - d + r > 0. Also R + d - r > 0. So it's positive. Similarly, (d + r)^2 - R^2 > 0 because d + r > R (since d > R - r). So both are positive. Good. So y0 is defined.

Now, we also have x0 + r = ((d + r)^2 - R^2) / (2 d). So that's one of the factors. Similarly, note that R^2 - (d - r)^2 = (R - d + r)(R + d - r). But we might also need (d + r)^2 - R^2 which appears.

Now, compute X = (d + R - r)/2. Then y_P = -X (x0 + r) / y0. But x0 + r = ((d + r)^2 - R^2) / (2 d). So

y_P = - \frac{ (d + R - r) }{2} * \frac{ ((d + r)^2 - R^2) }{2 d} / y0 = - \frac{ (d + R - r) ((d + r)^2 - R^2) }{4 d y0}.

That's messy, but maybe we can also express y_P in terms of something like \frac{ (d + R - r) (R^2 - (d - r)^2) }{4 d y0} times something? Actually note that ((d + r)^2 - R^2) appears in y0^2 as a factor, but y0 itself includes that square root. So y_P is proportional to ((d + r)^2 - R^2) / y0, and y0 is proportional to sqrt( ((d + r)^2 - R^2) * (R^2 - (d - r)^2) / (2d). So we can simplify: ((d + r)^2 - R^2) / y0 = ((d + r)^2 - R^2) / ( sqrt(((d + r)^2 - R^2) * (R^2 - (d - r)^2)) / (2d) ) = sqrt(((d + r)^2 - R^2)) * (2d) / sqrt( (R^2 - (d - r)^2) ). So

y_P = - \frac{ (d + R - r) }{4 d} * ( ((d + r)^2 - R^2) / y0 ) = - \frac{ (d + R - r) }{4 d} * \frac{ ((d + r)^2 - R^2) }{ y0 }.

But ((d + r)^2 - R^2) / y0 = \frac{ ((d + r)^2 - R^2) }{ \frac{ \sqrt{ ((d + r)^2 - R^2) \cdot (R^2 - (d - r)^2) } }{2 d} } = \frac{ ((d + r)^2 - R^2) \cdot 2 d }{ \sqrt{ ((d + r)^2 - R^2) \cdot (R^2 - (d - r)^2) } } = 2 d \cdot \frac{ \sqrt{ ((d + r)^2 - R^2) } }{ \sqrt{ R^2 - (d - r)^2 } }.

Thus

y_P = - \frac{ (d + R - r) }{4 d} * 2 d * \frac{ \sqrt{ ((d + r)^2 - R^2) } }{ \sqrt{ R^2 - (d - r)^2 } } = - \frac{ (d + R - r) }{2} * \frac{ \sqrt{ ((d + r)^2 - R^2) } }{ \sqrt{ R^2 - (d - r)^2 } }.

That is somewhat simpler: y_P = - \frac{ (d + R - r) \sqrt{ (d + r)^2 - R^2 } }{ 2 \sqrt{ R^2 - (d - r)^2 } }.

Compute:

PA^2 = ((c+d)/2 - 0)^2 + (y_P - a)^2.
PC^2 = ((c+d)/2 - c)^2 + (y_P - 0)^2 = ((d - c)/2)^2 + y_P^2.

Set equal:

((c+d)/2)^2 + (y_P - a)^2 = ((d - c)/2)^2 + y_P^2.

Expand:

((c+d)^2)/4 + y_P^2 - 2a y_P + a^2 = ((d - c)^2)/4 + y_P^2.

Cancel y_P^2:

((c+d)^2)/4 - 2a y_P + a^2 = ((d - c)^2)/4.

Thus 2a y_P = ((c+d)^2)/4 - ((d - c)^2)/4 + a^2 = ( (c+d)^2 - (d - c)^2 )/4 + a^2 = (4cd)/4 + a^2 = cd + a^2.

Therefore

y_P = \frac{cd + a^2}{2a}.

Let‚Äôs denote:

y_P = (cd + a^2) / (2a).

Now, we also have:

Similarly, n - x_P = n - (m - r + n + R)/2 = (2n - m + r - n - R)/2 = (n - m + r - R)/2.

Thus (x_P - m)(n - x_P) = ( (n - m - r + R) * (n - m + r - R) ) / 4.

Simplify the numerator: (u - r + R)*(u + r - R) where u = n - m. Compute:

Thus (x_P - m)(n - x_P) = \frac{ u^2 - (R - r)^2 }{ 4 }.

Now, u = n - m. So u^2 - (R - r)^2 = (n - m)^2 - (R - r)^2.

That's consistent with earlier.

Points:
B = (1/4, -‚ąö15/4)
E = (7/8, -‚ąö15/8)
F = (3/2, -‚ąö15/2)

We can try to find O such that distances equal. Since B and E are on the line x = something? B.x = 1/4, E.x = 7/8, F.x = 3/2. So they are not collinear. Let's compute O.

We can use coordinates with ‚ąö15. Let‚Äôs denote k = ‚ąö15. Then:

B = (1/4, -k/4)
E = (7/8, -k/8)
F = (3/2, -k/2)

Now, find the circumcenter. Use the method of solving linear equations from equal distances.

Compute differences:

For B and E:

e_x - b_x = 7/8 - 1/4 = 7/8 - 2/8 = 5/8.
e_y - b_y = (-k/8) - (-k/4) = -k/8 + k/4 = -k/8 + 2k/8 = k/8.

Thus vector (5/8, k/8). So (e_x - b_x, e_y - b_y) = (5/8, k/8). Then (e_x - b_x)^2 + (e_y - b_y)^2 = (25/64) + (k^2/64) = (25 + 15)/64 = 40/64 = 5/8.

Now, B and F:

f_x - b_x = 3/2 - 1/4 = 6/4 - 1/4 = 5/4.
f_y - b_y = (-k/2) - (-k/4) = -k/2 + k/4 = -k/4.

So vector (5/4, -k/4). Square: (25/16) + (k^2/16) = (25+15)/16 = 40/16 = 5/2.

Now, set up equations:

(O - B)^2 = (O - E)^2 ‚Üí (O_x - 1/4)^2 + (O_y + k/4)^2 = (O_x - 7/8)^2 + (O_y + k/8)^2.
Expand and simplify to get linear equation.

Compute:

Left: (O_x^2 - 2*(1/4) O_x + 1/16) + (O_y^2 + 2*(k/4) O_y + (k^2/16)).
Right: (O_x^2 - 2*(7/8) O_x + (49/64)) + (O_y^2 + 2*(k/8) O_y + (k^2/64)).

Cancel O_x^2, O_y^2. Then:

- (1/2) O_x + 1/16 + (k/2) O_y + 15/16 = - (7/4) O_x + 49/64 + (k/4) O_y + 15/64.

Combine constants: 1/16+15/16 = 1. So left constant = 1. Right constants: 49/64+15/64 = 64/64 = 1. So constants cancel nicely. So we have:

- (1/2) O_x + (k/2) O_y = - (7/4) O_x + (k/4) O_y.

Bring terms:

- (1/2) O_x + (7/4) O_x + (k/2) O_y - (k/4) O_y = 0 ‚Üí ( (-2/4 + 7/4) O_x ) + ( (2k/4 - k/4) O_y ) = 0 ‚Üí (5/4) O_x + (k/4) O_y = 0 ‚Üí Multiply by 4: 5 O_x + k O_y = 0. (Equation 1)

Now, equation from B and F:

(O - B)^2 = (O - F)^2 ‚Üí (O_x - 1/4)^2 + (O_y + k/4)^2 = (O_x - 3/2)^2 + (O_y + k/2)^2.

Expand:

Left: O_x^2 - (1/2) O_x + 1/16 + O_y^2 + (k/2) O_y + k^2/16.
Right: O_x^2 - 3 O_x + (9/4) + O_y^2 + k O_y + k^2/4.

Cancel O_x^2, O_y^2. Then:

- (1/2) O_x + 1/16 + (k/2) O_y + 15/16 = -3 O_x + 9/4 + k O_y + 15/4? Wait k^2 = 15, so k^2/4 = 15/4. Actually careful:

Left constant: 1/16 + k^2/16 = 1/16 + 15/16 = 1. Right constant: 9/4 + k^2/4 = 9/4 + 15/4 = 24/4 = 6.

So equation becomes:

- (1/2) O_x + (k/2) O_y + 1 = -3 O_x + k O_y + 6.

Bring all terms:

- (1/2) O_x + (k/2) O_y + 1 + 3 O_x - k O_y - 6 = 0 ‚Üí ( -1/2 + 3 ) O_x + ( k/2 - k ) O_y -5 = 0 ‚Üí (5/2) O_x + ( -k/2 ) O_y -5 = 0 ‚Üí Multiply by 2: 5 O_x - k O_y -10 = 0 ‚Üí 5 O_x - k O_y = 10. (Equation 2)

Now we have two linear equations:

(1) 5 O_x + k O_y = 0.
(2) 5 O_x - k O_y = 10.

Add them: 10 O_x = 10 ‚Üí O_x = 1. Subtract (1) from (2): (5 O_x - k O_y) - (5 O_x + k O_y) = 10 - 0 ‚Üí -2 k O_y = 10 ‚Üí O_y = -5 / k = -5/‚ąö15 = - (5‚ąö15)/15 = -‚ąö15/3 ‚Čą -1.291. But we need to produce a solution that is clear and rigorous. I'll try to outline a plan:

1. Set up coordinate system with M = (0,0), N = (d,0). (d > 0)
2. Let the circles have radii r and R, with r < R. Since they intersect, we have d > R - r and d < R + r.
3. The common chord AB is perpendicular to MN, so AB is vertical. Let A = (x0, y0) with y0 > 0, B = (x0, -y0). Then MA^2 = x0^2 + y0^2 = r^2, NA^2 = (x0 - d)^2 + y0^2 = R^2. Solve for x0: x0 = (d^2 + r^2 - R^2) / (2d). Also y0^2 = r^2 - x0^2.
4. The line MN (x-axis) intersects ő© at C and őď at D. Because the order C, M, N, D, we have C = ( -r, 0) (since M is at 0, and C is on opposite side from N), and D = (d + R, 0). (We need to confirm that D is on the same side of N as away from M, and that indeed D lies on őď. Since N is at (d,0), the circle őď intersects the x-axis at points with x = d ¬Ī R. The one to the right (greater than d) is d+R, to the left is d-R. Since N is between M and D, D must be to the right of N. So D = (d+R, 0). Good.)
5. Compute P, the circumcenter of triangle ACD. Since C and D lie on x-axis, we can compute P as follows: Let Q = midpoint of CD = (( -r + (d+R))/2, 0) = ( (d + R - r)/2, 0). The perpendicular bisector of CD is vertical line x = (d + R - r)/2. So P has x-coordinate X = (d + R - r)/2. Then we find y-coordinate from PA = PC.
   - PA^2 = (X - x0)^2 + (y_P - y0)^2.
   - PC^2 = (X + r)^2 + y_P^2.
   Equating yields relation: (X - x0)^2 + (y_P - y0)^2 = (X + r)^2 + y_P^2.
   Simplify to get: y_P = -X (x0 + r) / y0. (We derived earlier.)
   This is exact. We'll keep it.
6. Compute H, orthocenter of triangle PMN. M = (0,0), N = (d,0), P = (X, y_P). Since MN is horizontal, the altitude from P is vertical line x = X. So H lies on that line. The altitude from M is perpendicular to PN. Compute PN = (d - X, -y_P). A direction vector perpendicular to PN is (y_P, d - X) (since dot = (d - X)*y_P + (-y_P)*(d - X) = 0). We have y0^2 = r^2 - x0^2. Compute x0^2 = ((1 + r^2 - R^2)^2)/4. So

y0^2 = r^2 - \frac{(1 + r^2 - R^2)^2}{4} = \frac{4r^2 - (1 + r^2 - R^2)^2}{4}.

Now, note that (1 + r^2 - R^2)^2 = (R^2 - (1+r^2))^2. So

4r^2 - (1 + r^2 - R^2)^2 = 4r^2 - ( (R^2 - (1+r^2))^2 ). That seems messy. However, we also have that (X + r)^2 can be expressed. X = (1 + R - r)/2. So X + r = (1 + R - r)/2 + r = (1 + R + r)/2. So (X + r)^2 = (1 + R + r)^2 / 4.

Thus |v|^2 = (1 + R + r)^2 / 4 + X^2 (x0 + r)^2 / y0^2.

Now, X^2 = (1 + R - r)^2 / 4. So

|v|^2 = \frac{(1 + R + r)^2}{4} + \frac{(1 + R - r)^2 (x0 + r)^2}{4 y0^2}.

So |v|^2 = \frac{1}{4} \left[ (1 + R + r)^2 + \frac{(1 + R - r)^2 (x0 + r)^2}{y0^2} \right].

Now, we also have t_E = 2 r (X + r) / |v|^2 = 2 r * \frac{1 + R + r}{2} / |v|^2 = \frac{r (1 + R + r)}{|v|^2}.

And t_F = 2 (X (r+1) + r^2 - x0) / |v|^2.

Now, X (r+1) = \frac{1 + R - r}{2} * (r+1). So we need to compute X(r+1) + r^2 - x0. That's ( (1+R - r)(r+1)/2 ) + r^2 - x0. x0 is (1 + r^2 - R^2)/2.

So we can compute:

X(r+1) + r^2 - x0 = \frac{(1+R - r)(r+1)}{2} + r^2 - \frac{1 + r^2 - R^2}{2}.

Combine over denominator 2:

= \frac{(1+R - r)(r+1) + 2r^2 - (1 + r^2 - R^2)}{2}.

Simplify numerator:

(1+R - r)(r+1) = (1+R - r)*(r+1). Expand: (1)*(r+1) = r+1. (R)*(r+1) = Rr + R. (-r)*(r+1) = -r^2 - r. So sum: (r+1) + (Rr + R) + (-r^2 - r) = (r - r) + (1) + Rr + R - r^2 = 1 + Rr + R - r^2.

Thus (1+R - r)(r+1) = 1 + R + Rr - r^2. Wait we had 1 + Rr + R - r^2. That's 1 + R + Rr - r^2.

Now, add 2r^2: 1 + R + Rr - r^2 + 2r^2 = 1 + R + Rr + r^2.

Then subtract (1 + r^2 - R^2): (1 + R + Rr + r^2) - (1 + r^2 - R^2) = R + Rr + R^2.

So numerator = R + Rr + R^2 = R (1 + r + R). Thus

X(r+1) + r^2 - x0 = \frac{ R (1 + r + R) }{2}.

So t_F = 2 * \frac{ R (1 + r + R) }{2} / |v|^2 = \frac{ R (1 + r + R) }{|v|^2}.

Thus t_E = \frac{ r (1 + r + R) }{|v|^2}, t_F = \frac{ R (1 + r + R) }{|v|^2}.

Nice! So both t_E and t_F are multiples of (1+r+R). This is a simplification. So indeed:

We can try to simplify these using the formulas for x0 and X. Let's compute:

X = (d + R - r) / 2.
x0 = (d^2 + r^2 - R^2) / (2d).

We have (X + r) = (d + R - r + 2r)/2 = (d + R + r)/2.

So (X + r) is (d + r + R)/2. Good.

Now, compute d x0 = (d^2 + r^2 - R^2) / 2.

Then X (r + d) = ((d + R - r)/2) * (r + d). So

X (r + d) + r^2 - d x0 = \frac{(d + R - r)(d + r)}{2} + r^2 - \frac{d^2 + r^2 - R^2}{2}.

Combine over denominator 2:

= \frac{ (d + R - r)(d + r) + 2 r^2 - (d^2 + r^2 - R^2) }{2}.

Simplify numerator:

Compute (d + R - r)(d + r) = (d + r)*(d + R - r). Expand: (d)(d + R - r) = d^2 + dR - dr. (r)(d + R - r) = rd + rR - r^2. Sum = d^2 + dR - dr + rd + rR - r^2 = d^2 + dR + rR - r^2.

So (d + R - r)(d + r) = d^2 + (d + r)R - r^2.

Then add 2 r^2: d^2 + (d + r)R - r^2 + 2 r^2 = d^2 + (d + r)R + r^2.

Then subtract (d^2 + r^2 - R^2): (d^2 + (d + r)R + r^2) - (d^2 + r^2 - R^2) = (d + r)R + R^2.

Thus numerator = R (d + r) + R^2 = R (d + r + R). So

X (r + d) + r^2 - d x0 = \frac{ R (d + r + R) }{2}.

Therefore, t_F = 2 * \frac{ R (d + r + R) }{2} / |v|^2 = \frac{ R (d + r + R) }{|v|^2}.

Similarly, t_E = \frac{ 2 r (X + r) }{|v|^2} = \frac{ 2 r * \frac{d + r + R}{2} }{|v|^2} = \frac{ r (d + r + R) }{|v|^2}.

Thus we have:

t_E = \frac{ r T }{|v|^2}, \quad t_F = \frac{ R T }{|v|^2}, \quad \text{where } T = d + r + R.

So that's consistent with our earlier d=1 case.

Now, we also have that |v|^2 = PC^2 = (X + r)^2 + y_P^2. But we might find a simpler expression for |v|^2 using the fact that t_E |v|^2 = r T, so |v|^2 = \frac{ r T }{ t_E }. But t_E is unknown. But maybe we can find that O is the midpoint of the segment connecting the centers of the two circles? Not.

Let's try a different coordinate: Place the line AB as the x-axis, and the line MN as the y-axis. That's a common trick for two intersecting circles. Then the centers M and N lie on the y-axis. The circles intersect at A and B, which are symmetric about the y-axis. So we can set:

Let MN be the y-axis. Then M = (0, m), N = (0, n). The common chord AB is horizontal (since perpendicular to MN). So we can set A = (a, 0), B = (-a, 0) (or vice versa). However, we also derived that H = (X, X(d - X)/y_P) with d = MN. But also, the altitude from M is perpendicular to PN. That yields H's x-coordinate is something like (some expression). In our earlier calculation with M=(0,0), N=(d,0) we had H's x-coordinate = X. So we need to decide which orientation is easier.

## Solution
We adopt a coordinate system that simplifies the configuration.  Let the line of centers \(MN\) be the \(x\)-axis.  Because the two circles intersect, the common chord \(AB\) is perpendicular to \(MN\); hence we may place \(A\) and \(B\) symmetrically about the \(x\)-axis.  Moreover, we can scale the figure so that the distance \(MN\) becomes \(1\) (similarity invariance).  Thus we set

\[
M=(0,0),\qquad N=(1,0).
\]

Let the radii be \(\Omega\) with radius \(r\) and \(\Gamma\) with radius \(R\); we have \(0<r<R\) and the intersection condition \(|R-r|<1<R+r\).  The circles intersect at two points \(A\) and \(B\).  Because \(AB\perp MN\) and the midpoint of \(AB\) lies on \(MN\), we can write

\[
A=(x_0,y_0),\qquad B=(x_0,-y_0),\qquad y_0>0,
\]

with

\[
MA^2 = x_0^2+y_0^2 = r^2,\qquad NA^2 = (x_0-1)^2+y_0^2 = R^2.
\]

Solving these gives

\[
x_0 = \frac{1+r^2-R^2}{2},\qquad y_0^2 = r^2-x_0^2.
\]

The line \(MN\) (the \(x\)-axis) meets \(\Omega\) at \(C\) and \(\Gamma\) at \(D\).  The order \(C,M,N,D\) along the \(x\)-axis forces

\[
C = (-r,0),\qquad D = (1+R,0).
\]

Now consider the circumcenter \(P\) of \(\triangle ACD\).  Since \(C\) and \(D\) lie on the \(x\)-axis, the perpendicular bisector of \(CD\) is the vertical line through the midpoint of \(CD\).  The midpoint is

\[
Q = \left(\frac{-r+(1+R)}{2},0\right) = \left(\frac{1+R-r}{2},0\right).
\]

Let

\[
X = \frac{1+R-r}{2}.
\]

Thus \(P\) has \(x\)-coordinate \(X\).  Using \(PA = PC\) we find the \(y\)-coordinate \(y_P\):

\[
PA^2 = (X-x_0)^2 + (y_P-y_0)^2,\qquad PC^2 = (X+r)^2 + y_P^2.
\]

Equating and simplifying yields

\[
y_P = -\frac{X\,(x_0+r)}{y_0}.
\]

Hence

\[
P = (X,\; y_P).
\]

Next, let \(H\) be the orthocenter of \(\triangle PMN\).  Because \(M=(0,0)\) and \(N=(1,0)\), the side \(MN\) is horizontal.  The altitude from \(P\) is therefore vertical, so \(H\) lies on the line \(x = X\).  The altitude from \(M\) is perpendicular to \(PN\); a vector parallel to \(PN\) is

\[
\overrightarrow{PN} = (1-X,\; -y_P).
\]

A direction vector perpendicular to \(PN\) is \((y_P,\;1-X)\).  The line through \(M\) with this direction is \(\mathbf{t}\,(y_P,\;1-X)\).  Its intersection with \(x = X\) gives

\[
t\,y_P = X\quad\Longrightarrow\quad t = \frac{X}{y_P},
\]

and the corresponding \(y\)-coordinate is \(t\,(1-X) = \frac{X(1-X)}{y_P}\).  Consequently

\[
H = \left(X,\; \frac{X(1-X)}{y_P}\right).
\]

Now, let the line \(AP\) meet \(\Omega\) again at \(E\neq A\) and \(\Gamma\) again at \(F\neq A\).  The direction vector of \(AP\) is

\[
\mathbf{v} = P-A = (X-x_0,\; y_P-y_0).
\]

Parameterize the line \(AP\) by \(A + t\mathbf{v}\).  Because \(A\) lies on \(\Omega\) and \(\Gamma\), we have

\[
|A + t_E\mathbf{v}|^2 = r^2,\qquad |A + t_F\mathbf{v} - N|^2 = R^2,
\]

with \(t_E,t_F\) the parameters of \(E,F\) respectively.  Solving the first gives

\[
t_E = -\frac{2\,(A\cdot\mathbf{v})}{| \mathbf{v}|^2}.
\]

Since \(A\cdot\mathbf{v} = A\cdot(P-A) = A\cdot P - |A|^2\) and \(A\cdot P = -X r\) (as can be checked directly), we obtain

\[
A\cdot\mathbf{v} = -X r - r^2.
\]

Thus

\[
t_E = \frac{2X r + 2r^2}{|\mathbf{v}|^2} = \frac{2r\,(X+r)}{|\mathbf{v}|^2}.
\]

But \(X+r = \frac{1+R+r}{2}\) (because \(X = \frac{1+R-r}{2}\)), so

\[
t_E = \frac{r\,(1+r+R)}{|\mathbf{v}|^2}.
\]

Similarly, using the second intersection with \(\Gamma\) and the fact that \(A\) lies on \(\Gamma\), we get

\[
t_F = \frac{R\,(1+r+R)}{|\mathbf{v}|^2}.
\]

(Indeed, from \(|A + t\mathbf{v} - N|^2 = R^2\) and \(|A-N|^2=R^2\) one obtains \(t_F\,|\mathbf{v}|^2 = -2\,(A-N)\cdot\mathbf{v}\), and a direct simplification leads to the expression above.)

Denote \(T = 1+r+R\).  Then

\[
E = A + \frac{r T}{|\mathbf{v}|^2}\,\mathbf{v},\qquad
F = A + \frac{R T}{|\mathbf{v}|^2}\,\mathbf{v}.
\]

Now consider the circumcircle of \(\triangle BEF\).  Let \(O\) be its center.  Because \(B\) and \(E\) lie on \(\Omega\) and \(|B|^2 = |E|^2 = r^2\), we have

\[
|O-B|^2 = |O-E|^2 \;\Longrightarrow\; 2\,O\!\cdot\!(E-B) = |E|^2-|B|^2 = 0.
\]

Thus \(O\) is orthogonal to \(E-B\):

\[
O\!\cdot\!(E-B)=0.
\]

Write \(E-B = (A + \frac{r T}{|\mathbf{v}|^2}\mathbf{v}) - B = (A-B) + \frac{r T}{|\mathbf{v}|^2}\,\mathbf{v}\).

Since \(A-B = (0,\,2y_0)\) (because the \(x\)-coordinates are equal), this becomes

\[
O\!\cdot\!(A-B) + \frac{r T}{|\mathbf{v}|^2}\,O\!\cdot\!\mathbf{v}=0. \tag{1}
\]

We also know that the vector \(\mathbf{v}\) is not parallel to \(A-B\) (unless degenerate), so we can obtain another scalar relation.  The circumcenter \(O\) must satisfy

\[
|O-B|^2 = |O-A|^2 \;\Longrightarrow\; 2\,O\!\cdot\!(A-B) = |A|^2-|B|^2 = 0,
\]

which is already used.  A more convenient relation is obtained from the equality of distances to \(B\) and \(E\) (or \(B\) and \(F\)), but we will instead use the fact that \(O\) lies on the perpendicular bisector of \(AE\) and \(AF\).  The vector \(\mathbf{v}\) is a direction of \(AP\); because \(A\) is on both circles, we can compute \(O\!\cdot\!\mathbf{v}\).  Indeed, using \(|O-E|^2 = |O-B|^2\) and the expressions for \(E\) and \(B\) we derive

\[
O\!\cdot\!\mathbf{v} = A\!\cdot\!\mathbf{v} + \frac{r+R}{2}\,T. \tag{2}
\]

(The derivation: from \(|O-A|^2 = |O-E|^2\) we obtain \(2\,O\!\cdot\!(E-A) = |E|^2-|A|^2\); substituting \(E-A = \frac{r T}{|\mathbf{v}|^2}\,\mathbf{v}\) and simplifying yields (2).)

Now we have two linear equations for \(O\):

\[
O\!\cdot\!\mathbf{v} = \frac{R T}{2}, \qquad\text{and}\qquad O\!\cdot\!(A-B) = -\frac{r T}{|\mathbf{v}|^2}\,O\!\cdot\!\mathbf{v} = -\frac{r R T^2}{2|\mathbf{v}|^2}.
\]

(The first constant follows from (2) because \(A\!\cdot\!\mathbf{v} = -r T/2\) and \(\frac{r+R}{2}T = \frac{R T}{2}\) after cancellation; the second is obtained from (1) after inserting \(O\!\cdot\!\mathbf{v}\).)

Because \(A-B = (0,\,2y_0)\), we have \(O\!\cdot\!(A-B) = 2\,y_0\,O_y\).  Thus

\[
2\,y_0\,O_y = -\frac{r R T^2}{2|\mathbf{v}|^2}\quad\Longrightarrow\quad
O_y = -\frac{r R T^2}{4\,y_0\,|\mathbf{v}|^2}. \tag{3}
\]

Next, \(O\!\cdot\!\mathbf{v} = O_x\,v_x + O_y\,v_y = \frac{R T}{2}\).  Hence

\[
O_x\,v_x = \frac{R T}{2} - O_y\,v_y.
\]

Therefore

\[
O_x = \frac{ \frac{R T}{2} - O_y\,v_y }{ v_x }.
\]

Now we examine the line \(L\) through \(H\) parallel to \(AP\).  Its direction vector is \(\mathbf{v}\).  The distance from \(O\) to \(L\) is

\[
\operatorname{dist}(O,L) = \frac{| (O-H)\times \mathbf{v} |}{|\mathbf{v}|},
\]

where \(\times\) denotes the 2‚ÄĎdimensional cross product \(u\times w = u_x w_y - u_y w_x\).  We will show that this distance equals the radius \(R_{\triangle BEF}\) of the circle through \(B,E,F\).

The radius squared is \(R_{\triangle BEF}^2 = |O-B|^2\).  Because \(B\) and \(E\) lie on \(\Omega\), we have \(|B|^2 = |E|^2 = r^2\).  Also, from the expression for \(O\) we can compute \(|O-B|^2\) in a simpler way.  Notice that \(B\) is the reflection of \(A\) across the \(x\)-axis; hence \(B = (x_0,-y_0)\).  Using (3) and the expressions for \(v_x,v_y\) we can simplify the cross product.

We need to find a convenient expression for \(|\mathbf{v}|^2\).  Since \(\mathbf{v} = P-A\) and \(P\) is the circumcenter of \(\triangle ACD\), we have

\[
|\mathbf{v}|^2 = |P-A|^2.
\]

But we can also compute \(|\mathbf{v}|^2\) directly from the coordinates of \(A\) and \(P\):

\[
|\mathbf{v}|^2 = (X-x_0)^2 + (y_P-y_0)^2.
\]

A lengthy but straightforward simplification shows that

\[
|\mathbf{v}|^2 = \frac{ (1+r+R)^2 }{4} + \frac{ (1+R-r)^2\,(x_0+r)^2 }{4\,y_0^2}.
\]

However, the crucial observation is that the combination

\[
\frac{|\mathbf{v}|^2}{\,T\,} \quad\text{and}\quad \frac{1}{|\mathbf{v}|^2}
\]

will appear in the expressions, and we can avoid computing \(|\mathbf{v}|^2\) explicitly by using the following trick.  From the expressions for \(t_E\) and \(t_F\) we have

\[
t_E\,|\mathbf{v}|^2 = r T,\qquad t_F\,|\mathbf{v}|^2 = R T.
\]

Therefore

\[
\frac{1}{|\mathbf{v}|^2} = \frac{t_E}{r T} = \frac{t_F}{R T}.
\]

Now, we will compute \((O-H)\times\mathbf{v}\) in terms of the known vectors and then simplify.

We have

\[
H = \left( X,\; \frac{X(1-X)}{y_P} \right).
\]

Recall that \(X = \frac{1+R-r}{2}\), so \(X(1-X) = \frac{ (1+R-r)(1 - \frac{1+R-r}{2}) }{?} \).  Actually compute:

\[
X(1-X) = X - X^2.
\]

We will need to handle this expression.

We also have \(O_y\) from (3).  The cross product becomes

\[
(O-H)\times\mathbf{v} = (O_x - X)\,v_y - (O_y - y_H)\,v_x.
\]

Substituting \(O_x\) from the linear relation, we obtain

\[
(O_x - X)\,v_y = \frac{ \frac{R T}{2} - O_y v_y - X v_x }{ v_x }\,v_y.
\]

Thus

\[
(O-H)\times\mathbf{v} = \frac{ \frac{R T}{2} - O_y v_y - X v_x }{ v_x }\,v_y - (O_y - y_H) v_x.
\]

Now, we also have the relation \(O_y v_y\) can be expressed.  Because \(O_y\) is known in terms of \(|\mathbf{v}|^2\) and \(y_0\), and \(v_y\) is \(y_P - y_0\).  Maybe we can compute \(O_y v_y\) in a simpler form.  Let's attempt a different route.

We can compute the distance from \(O\) to \(L\) using the formula for the distance from a point to a line when the line is given by two points.  Since \(L\) passes through \(H\) with direction \(\mathbf{v}\), we can also express it as the set of points \(P\) such that \((P-H)\times\mathbf{v}=0\).  The distance from \(O\) to \(L\) is

\[
\frac{|(O-H)\times\mathbf{v}|}{|\mathbf{v}|}.
\]

Now, note that \(O\) is the circumcenter of \(BEF\), and \(B\) is symmetric of \(A\) about the \(x\)-axis.  Perhaps we can find a relationship between \(O\) and \(H\) that makes the cross product simple.  Let's compute with the explicit numeric example to see a pattern.  In that example, \(O=(1,- \sqrt{15}/3)\), \(H=(1.5,- \sqrt{15}/10)\), \(\mathbf{v}=(5/4,-3\sqrt{15}/4)\).  Then \(O-H = (-0.5,- \sqrt{15}/3 + \sqrt{15}/10) = (-0.5,-7\sqrt{15}/30)\).  The cross product with \(\mathbf{v}\) gave \(2\sqrt{15}/3\).  That's interesting.

Now, maybe we can compute \((O-H)\times\mathbf{v}\) using the equations we have.  Let's denote \(U = \mathbf{v}\).  We have \(O\cdot U = \frac{R T}{2}\).  Also, \(O\cdot (A-B) = -\frac{r R T^2}{2|\mathbf{v}|^2}\).  But we need \(O\times U = O_x v_y - O_y v_x\).  That is not directly given.  However, we can also compute \(O\times U\) using the fact that \(O\) is the circumcenter of \(BEF\) and that \(B\) and \(E\) are on \(\Omega\).  There is a known formula: If a circle passes through points \(B\) and \(E\) on \(\Omega\), then the circumcenter \(O\) lies on the perpendicular bisector of \(BE\).  But we already used that to get \(O\cdot (E-B)=0\).  That's a scalar equation.  For the cross product, we might compute \(O\times (E-B) = O\times (A-B) + \frac{r T}{|\mathbf{v}|^2} O\times \mathbf{v}\).  That might be messy.

Let's try to compute the distance from \(O\) to \(L\) by projecting \(O\) onto the line direction.  Since \(L\) is parallel to \(\mathbf{v}\), the distance is \(|(O-H)\times \mathbf{v}|/|\mathbf{v}|\).  But we also have that \(H\) is the orthocenter of \(PMN\).  Maybe we can find that \(O\) is the midpoint of the segment connecting \(P\) and the reflection of something across \(MN\).  Actually, in the numeric example, \(O = (1, -1.291)\) and \(P = (1.5, -1.9365)\).  The midpoint of \(OP\) is \((1.25, -1.6138)\).  That's not \(H\).  But perhaps \(O\) is the orthocenter of some triangle related to \(PMN\).  Let's compute the orthocenter of \(PMN\): \(H = (1.5, -0.387)\).  The centroid of \(PMN\) is something else.

Given the time, I'll present a solution that uses coordinates but with a more systematic algebraic simplification, culminating in the identity that the distance equals the radius.  I'll aim to be clear and rigorous, even if the algebra is long.  However, we need to produce a solution within this environment.  I'll attempt to derive the tangency condition in a clean way, perhaps by computing the power of \(H\) with respect to \((BEF)\) and relating it to something like \(HT^2\) where \(T\) is the tangency point.  But we don't know \(T\).

I realize that we can prove the tangency by showing that the line through \(H\) parallel to \(AP\) is the polar of \(A\) with respect to the circle \((BEF)\).  Then because \(A\) lies on the circle?  No, \(A\) is not on that circle.  But we can consider the circle through \(B,E,F\) and the line \(L\).  There's a known fact: In a circle, the line through the orthocenter of a triangle formed by the center and the two points where the line of centers meets the circles is tangent to the circle through the other intersection and those two points.  I'm not comfortable.

Let's try to compute \(O\) and then the distance explicitly in terms of the coordinates of \(A\) and \(P\), and then simplify using the relationships.  I'll denote:

- \(M = (0,0), N = (1,0)\).
- \(A = (a, b)\) where \(a = x_0, b = y_0\).
- \(P = (X, Y)\) where \(X = \frac{1+R-r}{2}, Y = -\frac{X(a+r)}{b}\).
- \(v = (X - a, Y - b)\).

We also have \(T = 1 + r + R\).

Now, compute \(O\) as the intersection of perpendicular bisectors.  The perpendicular bisector of \(BE\) and \(BF\) are lines.  Perhaps we can find \(O\) by solving:

\[
|O - B|^2 = |O - E|^2 \quad\text{and}\quad |O - B|^2 = |O - F|^2.
\]

We already used the first to get \(O \cdot (E - B) = 0\).  The second gives:

\[
|O - B|^2 = |O - F|^2 \;\Longrightarrow\; -2 O \cdot B + |B|^2 = -2 O \cdot F + |F|^2 \;\Longrightarrow\; 2 O \cdot (F - B) = |F|^2 - |B|^2.
\]

Now, \(F - B = (A + \frac{R T}{|\mathbf{v}|^2} \mathbf{v}) - B\).  That is messy.  But we can also use \( |O - E|^2 = |O - F|^2 \).  That gives:

\[
2 O \cdot (F - E) = |F|^2 - |E|^2.
\]

Now, \(F - E = \frac{R T}{|\mathbf{v}|^2} \mathbf{v} - \frac{r T}{|\mathbf{v}|^2} \mathbf{v} = \frac{(R - r) T}{|\mathbf{v}|^2} \mathbf{v}\).  So

\[
2 O \cdot \left( \frac{(R - r) T}{|\mathbf{v}|^2} \mathbf{v} \right) = |F|^2 - |E|^2.
\]

But \( |E|^2 = r^2 \) and \( |F|^2 \) is not directly known.  However, we can compute \( |F|^2 \) using the fact that \(F\) lies on \(\Gamma\) and that \(A\) is on \(\Gamma\).  That might give a relation.  But perhaps we can avoid this by noting that we already have two linear equations for \(O\) from the conditions that \(O\) is equidistant from \(B\) and \(E\) (zero dot product) and from \(B\) and \(F\) (some dot product).  But we need to express \(O\) in a simple form.

Alternatively, we can compute \(O\) by solving the system:

\[
\begin{cases}
O \cdot v = \frac{R T}{2},\\
O \cdot (A - B) = -\frac{r R T^2}{2 |\mathbf{v}|^2}.
\end{cases}
\]

This is a linear system.  If we express \(O\) in the basis \(\{v, A-B\}\), we can solve.  The matrix is:

\[
\begin{pmatrix}
v_x & v_y \\
0 & 2b
\end{pmatrix}
\]

with unknown \(O = (O_x, O_y)\).  Actually we have two scalar equations, but they involve dot products.  They are linear in \(O\).  We can solve by treating \(O\) as unknown vector.  Write:

\[
v_x O_x +
